I like the snap on brush heads for these brushes.  These are great brushes and do a great job cleaning, but I think they are too expensive and they include features I never used (and would bet most people don&#x27;t use).&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;When this one died, I decided to go with the most basic Sonicare toothbrush that had the quadpacer and this style of snap-on brush head. At the time of this review, that ends up being the Gum Health Sonicare, which is $80 vs. the Flexcare at $130.  So far, I&#x27;m happy with the gum health toothbrush, but it&#x27;s only been a week. It&#x27;s not quite as sleek as the Flexcare, but given that I never used the advanced features of the Flexcare, I feel like I&#x27;ve made a smarter purchase this time around.  We&#x27;ll see how long it lasts though.</li>

    <li> I upgraded from an Elite 7300 to this model and I am glad I did.  The slimmer handle takes just a little getting used to if you are used to a bigger handle but I really like the streamlined feel to it.  The toothbrush attachment is so easy to afix to the base and the toothbrush head itself seems to reach much better.  There are several choices for cleaning, including shorter and longer brushing cycles as well as massage and gentle options.  I am experimenting with all of them and the main button is easy to use and it is very simple once you get in the habit to select which option you want.  If you like to stick with the same type of cleaning every day than it will do just that as well. The toothbrush is quieter than my old model and it really feels less abrasive even though it does a great job of cleaning.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;I did not buy the model that had the sanitizer because I knew I would never get around to using that part so I did not want to pay for a feature that I most likely would not use. With that said, the charger for just the toothbrush is fairly small and does not require lining up the toothbrush like some previous models.  I am very happy with this toothbrush.</li>
    <li> This is easily the best toothbrush, manual or electric, that I have ever had. My dentist recommends that I brush without toothpaste for better cleaning using the two minute routine.  Then I use the go care (1 min total) setting to bush with toothpaste. My teeth feel fresh and smooth overnight when I brush before bed. I need to follow a routine the next morning or I will forget to brush.  Amazing machine.</li>
    <li> Everyone should use a sonicare...it cleans teeth and gums like nothing else. I will never go back to an ordinary toothbrush.</li>
    <li> The new sonicare toothbrush is very different from my well over 10 year old model.  It works probably better than the older one.  I was surprised that it was not sold with at least one more spare brush.  Anyway, it does what it is supposed to do and seems easy to travel with.</li>
    <li> This toothbrush does an excellent job of teeth cleaning.  However, it continues Phillips tradition of making poorly engineered parts that are non repairable and designed to fall apart so you have to buy new ones frequently.  In my case, this toothbrush failed after about six months.  When I went to take it apart to safely discard the environmentally polluting (read CHEAP) battery per instructions I found the failure was simply a screw falling out of the vibrating mechanism. Per Phillips instructions, the process of taking it apart to repair it or replace a battery is non recoverable (ie destructive).  However, I was able to put the screw back and put it back together with some effort. In summary, using a non changeable poor quality battery and depending upon a screw to hold the vibrating mechanism together is such bad engineering it can&#x27;t be anything but purposefully designed in obsolescence.[[ASIN:B0013TVEWA Philips Sonicare FlexCare Rechargeable Sonic Toothbrush]][[ASIN:B000UK2W4C Philips Sonicare FlexCare Rechargeable Sonic Toothbrush with Sanitizer]]</li>
    <li> My daughter gave me a Sonicare toothbrush 8 years ago that I used until I \\&quot;traded it in\\&quot; for a newer model. The brush handle is slimmer, the recharger less bulky, the toothbrush head formed for easier manipulation on those back molars. The Sonicare brushes are essential to my good oral health.  I appreciate the FlexCare options of the brush and use the different modes for different needs .  Good choice for me to upgrade.</li>
    <li> To give you some context, I&#x27;ve owned two Sonicare toothbrushes and four Oral-B toothbrushes over the last 17 years, including the ever popular 3D/8850 and the \\&quot;Triumph\\&quot; 4000/5000/9000 (whatever number they&#x27;ve decided to use this year).  I have never used the Oral-B sonic brush, mostly because it has mediocre reviews and requires different brush-heads anyway.  If I&#x27;m going to invest in all new equipment, I might as well buy the one with 4.5 stars...  I have found this particular Sonicare to be the best toothbrush I&#x27;ve ever used, and not necessarily for the reasons you might expect.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;First, what attracted me to Oral-B in the first place: it does a good job cleaning teeth and the standard brush-head is a very small, circular brush.  I always found the enlarged brush-heads or cross-action brush-heads too gimicky, uncomfortable, and less effective.  I would usually get 10 days to a charge, most brushes came with hard PLASTIC travel cases, which prevented them from being switched on inadvertently and discharging during the outbound flight of your vacation/business trip.  In short, after 17 years of using several Oral-B brushes, I would consider myself a loyal customer.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Cons of the last couple of Oral-B brushes can be found in the reviews I&#x27;ve written on the relevant pages, although it&#x27;s hard to keep up since they keep switching model numbers to confuse the customer.  Basically, there are three types still available: a very basic version that has the oldest style of charge and a chunky handle, a newer version with 40,000 revolutions per minute AND 3D back-and-forth motion (oscillation) AND the \\&quot;Triumph,\\&quot; which has the same specifications but an even chunkier handle to accommodate the LCD display.  Either of the latter two are fine, although the charging circuit for my Triumph failed after barely a year, which is what made my mind up to switch to Sonicare.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Pros of the Sonicare are manifold: it&#x27;s smaller, lighter, quieter, simpler, can be had with \\&quot;compact\\&quot; or regular brush heads.  The best feature for me is that it holds a charge for two full weeks, even though it&#x27;s so much lighter.  The weight may seem insignificant, but it all adds up when you travel on a daily basis.  Then there&#x27;s the results: my teeth are definitely cleaner.  That absolutely surprised me, given how gung-ho I&#x27;ve been about Oral-B all these years.  My teeth are smoother, whiter, and I&#x27;ve noticed that the red wine stains that usually affect my teeth between dental cleanings have disappeared, without the need for a dental cleaning.  Is the difference huge?  No, but it is noticeable, and it was a pleasant surprise.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;I thought the Sonicare and Oral-B would be very similar user experiences: both brushes have the little cut-outs every 30 seconds to remind you to change the set of teeth that you&#x27;re currently brushing.  It&#x27;s not too intrusive and I&#x27;ve always liked it.  This Sonicare completely cuts out after 2 minutes, unless you press the little button in advance to give you 3 minutes or 1 minute.  I don&#x27;t mind, since 2 minutes is all I ever shoot for (and you can always turn it right back on if you really need to).  The battery indicator has 3 little levels, similar to the Triumph.  So, objectively, they both clean teeth and have almost identical bells and whistles.  Subjectively, they feel very different (different brushing motions) and have different results.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;So why not 5 stars?  Well, this particular model came with a soft cloth travel case, which I fear will lead to discharging (first trip with it is scheduled for next week - I&#x27;ll be bringing the included compact charger with me).  Also, I can&#x27;t get to the very back of my teeth quite as easily as I could with the small circular Oral-B brush.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;To Oral-B, I would say you just lost a customer because you couldn&#x27;t be bothered to update your brushes with Lithium Ion batteries.  To everyone else, I&#x27;d say if you&#x27;re choosing between the two, the latest Sonicare brushes beat Oral-B on every dimension..</li>
    <li> works mechanically great,  but the brushes are very stiff and even when you buy &amp;#34;their&amp;#34; soft ones,  they are still too stiff for sensitive gums.</li>
    <li> I got this toothbrush right at a year ago when I learned that excessive crunching and sawing with my manual toothbrush had resulted in my need for a $1,100 torture procedure a.k.a a gum graft.  My new periodontist swore by the Philips and said that he&#x27;d been using one for 15 years.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;So I bought the FlexCare because I liked the idea of the sensitive setting.  At first everything went well. Then, I too, encountered the vibrating brush head that wobbles off when I first changed to a new brush head. Additionally, the model I purchased has 5 cleaning modes:  Clean (standard 2 minute clean), Gum Care (2 minute clean, followed by 1 minute for getting back to problem areas), Refresh (1 minute &#x27;quick&#x27; clean), Sensitive (2 minutes, reduced speed for sensitive teeth/gums), and Massage (for stimulating gums).  I use the &#x27;Clean&#x27; mode exclusively, and it works quite well.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;As others have mentioned, it is important that you do not power on the toothbrush until it is in your mouth.  Failing to do so will result in toothpaste all over.  BUT, if youve used electric toothbrushes before, you should already know this.. Also, don&#x27;t remove it from your mouth while its on, or you&#x27;ll experience the same issue.  One of the things that bothers me slightly is that occasionally I will hit the shaft of the brush head against one of my teeth, and its not the most pleasant experience -- it doesnt hurt, its just annoying when it happens.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;I wanted to post this review to bring up two things that have been mentioned in many of the reviews I have read that Philips seems to have fixed:&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;1) Changing the mode while brushing -- many people posted that it was possible (and very easy) to inadvertently change the mode while brushing due to the fact that the mode button is right near where your hand would be holding the brush.  On the model that I purchased, you cannot change the mode while the brush is on -- you must power it off first, fixing that first problem.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;2) Remembering the previous mode used -- while I use the &#x27;Clean&#x27; mode exclusively, other reviewers complained that the unit should remember the last mode used.  Some people prefer to use the &#x27;Sensitive&#x27; mode all the time, and found it annoying to have to change to their preferred mode everytime they used the toothbrush.  This problem appears to be corrected also.  Each time I have started the toothbrush, it has been in the mode that was last used -- not &#x27;Clean&#x27;.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Overall, the product is excellent and is a good investment for anyone that is aiming to improve their dental health.  I will update this review in a few months to let people know how it works out in the long run.ric toothbrushes before, you should already know this.. Also, don&#x27;t remove it from your mouth while its on, or you&#x27;ll experience the same issue.  One of the things that bothers me slightly is that occasionally I will hit the shaft of the brush head against one of my teeth, and its not the most pleasant experience -- it doesnt hurt, its just annoying when it happens.    I wanted to post this review to bring up two things that have been mentioned in many of the reviews I have read that Philips seems to have fixed:    1) Changing the mode while brushing -- many people posted that it was possible (and very easy) to inadvertently change the mode while brushing due to the fact that the mode button is right near where your hand would be holding the brush.  On the model that I purchased, you cannot change the mode while the brush is on -- you must power it off first, fixing that first problem.    2) Remembering the previous mode used -- while I use the &#x27;Clean&#x27; mode exclusively, other reviewers complained that the unit should remember the last mode used.  Some people prefer to use the &#x27;Sensitive&#x27; mode all the time, and found it annoying to have to change to their preferred mode everytime they used the toothbrush.  This problem appears to be corrected also.  Each time I have started the toothbrush, it has been in the mode that was last used -- not &#x27;Clean&#x27;.    Overall, the product is excellent and is a good investment for anyone that is aiming to improve their dental health.  I will update this review in a few months to let people know how it works out in the long run.</li>

    <li> The Philips Sonicare toothbrush range utilises ultrasonic cleaning (as used in hospital sterile service units to clean the gunk off surgical instruments) in addition to the physical vibration of the bristles on the teeth. You don&#x27;t need to move or agitate the brush: just hold it to the teeth upper, lower, left, right, inside, outside then rinse when finished. A timer is incorporated which issues an audible signal every 30 seconds, repeated four times to ensure upper left, upper right, lower left and lower right areas are cleaned properly.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;We&#x27;ve had other makes of electric toothbrush in the past which use the word \\&quot;sonic\\&quot; in the product description but few actually employ genuine ultrasonic cleaning technology like these from Philips (the Oral B range also uses genuine ultrasonic cleaning).&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The heads are removable for transportation and may be changed or renewed regularly, and in our case there have been no issues with the head working loose as reported by a minority of reviewers here. If you have two toothbrushes in the bathroom, the handles come colour-coded so you don&#x27;t get mixed up. However if you do have two brushes, an irritating minor issue will soon become evident.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The instructions for use insist the toothbrushes should be kept on the charging base (a clever device which works well and only exposes sealed plastic to sealed plastic, so is effectively waterproof). However keeping both brushes on the charging base is simply not possible as no twin charger is available, so if you have two brushes then they have to be charged alternately. Almost no bathroom is fitted with more than one electric razor/toothbrush power socket, so buying two separate chargers will not solve this problem. This is a minor design fault, as mentioned by another reviewer, which quickly becomes obvious (and slightly irksome) to the new user.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Another consideration is that these are almost the most expensive electric toothbrushes on the market, but worth it if you value good dental hygiene. Again, in our case they have proved to be 100% reliable in service so over time the initial outlay evens out.dental hygiene. Again, in our case they have proved to be 100% reliable in service so over time the initial outlay evens out.</li>

    <li> I&#x27;ve been using an older Sonicare model, the \\&quot;Essence 5300\\&quot; on which the switch was beginning to go out.  I decided to upgrade to a Flexcare model and am very glad I did.  The Flexcare has been a great improvement all around.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Pros:&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;-New Lighter, Slimmer Handle&lt;br /&gt;This is probably the first thing you&#x27;ll notice if you&#x27;re coming from an older Sonicare handle, and it is a very welcome improvement.  The FlexCare handle is small and feels much better in the hand than my old bulky 5300 handle.  The unit is actually longer than my old brush, but that&#x27;s not a big deal at all.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;-New ProResults Brush Head system&lt;br /&gt;The new heads are great.  Sonicare moved the tuning fork from the head to the handle, which means smaller brush heads than on the old models.  A 3 pack of the new heads is the same price as a 2 pack of the old heads, making them actually a better deal for a superior product.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;-No More Crud in the Handle!&lt;br /&gt;The biggest problem with my old brush was that old toothpaste/drool/water tended to collected in the space where the handle connects to the brush head.  It would get all moldy and gross if you didn&#x27;t clean it out regularly.  Thankfully, this problem is completely gone with the Flexcare handles.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;-Lithium Ion Batteries&lt;br /&gt;I&#x27;ve waited a long time for Sonicare to upgrade from inferior NiCd batteries, and they finally have.  With Lithium Ion, you don&#x27;t have to worry about battery capacity decreasing over time, or a memory effect.  The new batteries charge faster, too.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;-Quadpacer&lt;br /&gt;This is basically a beep that goes off every 30 seconds while brushing, reminding you to switch to a new area of your mouth.  It has appeared in previous models (not my 5300 though), and is nice if you&#x27;ll use it, but I tend to go at my own pace as I focus more on problem areas in my mouth and less on others.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;-5 Total Brushing Modes/Routines&lt;br /&gt;There are 3 selectable \\&quot;modes\\&quot;: clean mode: (2 minutes broken into 30 second segments), sensitive (a \\&quot;gentler\\&quot; clean mode) and massage (the brush pulsates for gum stimulation).  Go Care Routine shortines the standard 2 minute clean mode to 1 minute, while Max Care is a longer, 3 minute cycle that includes both clean and massage mode.  I set the brush on clean mode and tend not to use the other options, but some may find them useful.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;-More Environmentally Friendly&lt;br /&gt;Although buying a new product of any sort will always have a negative environmental footprint, these new brushes are better than the old models due to their Li-Ion batteries, reduced bulk, and lower waste in the smaller replacement brush heads.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Cons:&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;-Price&lt;br /&gt;Let&#x27;s be clear on this: a Sonicare toothbrush is a luxury product.  You&#x27;re paying a lot more up front, and you get to keep paying more every time you buy replacement heads.  Surprisingly, heads for Flexcare units are actually a cheaper than the older models; you get 3 Flexcare heads for the same price as 2 of the old heads.  The per head price is still several times what it would cost to buy a new manual brush; but for me the superior cleaning is worth it.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Noise&lt;br /&gt;-Flexcare models are louder than older models.  I&#x27;m not sure why this is, and supposedly vibration has been reduced in exchange, but the noise can be too much for some.  The UV sanitizer also makes a quiet electric \\&quot;hum\\&quot; when plugged in with the door closed, even if the UV bulb isn&#x27;t run.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;-Buttons can be depressed while brushing&lt;br /&gt;A couple of people have complained about accidentally hitting 1 of the 2 buttons on the handle while brushing.  It hasn&#x27;t happened to me, but for completeness I&#x27;ll include this as a possible con.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Overall:&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;This brush is a vast improvement over my previous brush, and has fixed most of the problems I had without introducing any significant new ones.  The fact that replacement brush heads are actually cheaper than on my old brush is a big plus.  I highly recommend the Flexcare brushes as a great upgrade to any toothbrush, electric or manual, that you&#x27;re currently using.</li>

Didn&#x27;t think I&#x27;d use it much but I have quite a few times, and it&#x27;s perfect for when I do.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Comfortable to hold as well, and easy to keep clean. Still looks like new and all I ever do is rinse it off for a few seconds after each use.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Teeth and gums also feel great after brushing, much better than a toothbrush. I did use a normal toothbrush on a handful of occasions over the past year and I definitely feel that even when I brush for longer, I never feel I&#x27;m getting &#x27;quite as clean&#x27; of a result compared to the electric. Or maybe I just lost my toothbrush technique/comfort, certainly possible.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;There is of course no substantiated proof that it is better to brush with an electric. That said though, my dentist has complimented me on particularly good teeth/gum cleaning and health over the past year, but that&#x27;s more likely due to the convenience and effectiveness of this brush making it easier for me to stick to the habit. Either way, it&#x27;s definitely a good thing in favor of the brush.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Lastly, brush heads seem to last longer/perform better than past electric brushes I&#x27;ve used. I&#x27;ve been perfectly content going through about one head every quarter, which at ~$20-30/year isn&#x27;t bad!&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;__________________________________________&lt;br /&gt;THE BAD:&lt;br /&gt;I will agree with another reviewer, in that for the price you&#x27;re really not getting much improvement over a typical electric brush you could likely find for a good $30-$50 cheaper. I used a ~$40 Brawn electric brush for 2 years before this, and there wasn&#x27;t too much of a difference in upgrading to this (despite the price being much higher). Although, it does appear they&#x27;ve lowered the price quite a bit over the past year, which I definitely feel is appropriate.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Additionally, I found the other modes for brushing rather useless. I played with it for a while, but at the end of the day turning tooth brushing into a project wasn&#x27;t for me. I look more for the quick, convenient, result.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;__________________________________________&lt;br /&gt;IN SHORT:&lt;br /&gt;Pros:&lt;br /&gt;- Durable, reliable, long-lasting.&lt;br /&gt;- Good, long-lasting battery, and easy to charge.&lt;br /&gt;- Comfortable to hold and use, easy to clean.&lt;br /&gt;- Convenient travel case included.&lt;br /&gt;- Excellent in cleaning teeth/gums.&lt;br /&gt;- Replacement brush heads of good quality and value.&lt;br /&gt;- Helps keep teeth-brushing &#x60;fun&#x27; and as less of a chore, making it easier to develop and keep good dental hygiene habits.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Cons:&lt;br /&gt;- Price is much higher than other mostly equivalent models (but who knows, maybe they would&#x27;ve crapped out by now?).&lt;br /&gt;- Extra settings/modes relatively useless (subjective, of course).&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Look forward to getting another 1-2 years out of this before considering an upgrade, and will definitely aim for Sonicare again. Happy customer.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;***EDIT 10/11/2012&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;This, bumps the product up to a 5-star from my original 4-star.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;I&#x27;m about a month away from the 2-year mark, and I started to experience problems with the brush. The vibration was losing strength, and causing a loud grinding noise when any pressure was applied. It still worked as normal for a while, but progressively got worse to the point where the brush was hardly vibrating at all. This was incredibly discouraging as it wasn&#x27;t all that old of a toothbrush (and expensive!), but I caved and was willing to repurchase a new one as I was very happy with it otherwise.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Low and behold though, before purchasing I noticed a 2-year warranty.     <li> This toothbrush really does a great job of cleaning even with a soft brush, so long as you apply a bit of pressure. The one drawback is the built-in timer that, so far as i can tell, cannot be turned off.  The company has decided how long you should brush and to carry out their intentions, the timing mechanism beeps once a minute. It&#x27;s not the sound that is so objectionable but rather the fact that the beep slightly zaps the brush off your tooth surface, breaking your rhythm and causing the brush to miss the spot it was about to brush. This happens twice per cycle and after three minutes the brush automatically turns off.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;I don&#x27;t appreciate that my toothbrush has that much of a mind of its own. I would much prefer making my own brushing decisions!</li>
    <li> This Sonicare electric toothbrush is, simply put, an excellent product.  I&#x27;ve been using a Braun (which I believe is the same as Oral B) electric toothbrush for years, and it does not compare with this newest generation of dental hygiene products.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;This model comes preset with a step-up program that gradually increases the speed of the brush head over 14 uses until it reaches maximum effectiveness, a feature designed especially to those new to electric toothbrushes.  Although I&#x27;ve used an electric toothbrush for years, I decided to try out the pre-programmed sessions to see how it worked.  On the first day, the toothbrush sounded like a bee in my mouth; it was gentle, with enough high-speed vibration to clean my teeth better than my old, full-speed Braun.  Over the fourteen uses -- I did it in a week, but those new to electric toothbrushes might want to use it only once a day at first -- the brush lost its whine and gradually moved to its final, more authoritative vibration that cleans exceptionally well.  Even at maximum power, this toothbrush does not vibrate through my entire skull the way my Braun does.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The coolest part of the Sonicare is the option of selecting different modes.  The sensitive teeth setting is more like a mosquito than a bee; although I have sensitive teeth, I find that the regular cleaning doesn&#x27;t bother me and is more thorough.  You  may select \\&quot;Go Care\\&quot; (a one-minute touch up between regular brushings), \\&quot;Massage\\&quot; for the gums, and MaxCare for a 30 second tooth brushing following by a fifteen second gum massage for each quadrant (total time &#x3D; 3 minutes.)  To remind you to switch \\&quot;quadrants\\&quot; of your mouth, the pitch changes slightly.  After the routine time is up, the toothbrush stops.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;First time electric toothbrush users should note the instructions about angling the brush head and moving gently side to side.  You do not move the brush much yourself as you would with a traditional tooth brush; otherwise, you&#x27;ll irritate or even rip your gums.  My dentist has always advocated using an electric toothbrush once a day, not twice, with a traditional brush doing the work for the second brushing, although I find that the Sonicare, unlike my old Braun, is gentle enough to use twice.  Still, users might not want to throw out their toothbrushes.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The Sonicare comes with a stand charger, one standard brush head, user identification bands, a soft carrying case, and a plastic cap to protect the brush head while traveling.  Those who have smaller mouths will want to buy the optional [[ASIN:B000FEF1VO Philips Sonicare Elite (E-Series) Replacement Brush Head, Compact (2-Pack)]] since it&#x27;s not easy maneuvering the head behind, say, the front teeth.  If you are concerned about sanitizing the heads, you might prefer to buy the version with the sanitizer ([[ASIN:B0012AOO1I Philips Sonicare Flexcare Professional with UV Sanitizer]]), although you can always buy a sanitizer separately later ([[ASIN:B000V5Z4J6 Philips Sonicare UV Sanitizer]].)&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;I have only two complaints about this product:  you cannot attach it to the wall to keep counter space free, and the automatic stop feature is annoying if I&#x27;m not quite done brushing. The inability to mount it on the wall is especially frustrating since the handle is incredibly easy to knock off the charger.  Some people might not like the fact that it tickles like the Dickens when you hit the roof of your mouth while on the brushing speeds.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;I highly recommend this toothbrush. Electric toothbrushes promote oral health through both cleaner teeth and gum stimulation, and the Sonicare is an exceptional entry into the market.our gums.     <li> This Sonicare brush has an auto shutoff function after two minutes (recommended brush-time length), and at each 30-second interval it will stop for a brief second to let you know to \\&quot;switch quadrants\\&quot;.  There is also a silver button you can push to select \\&quot;Sensitive\\&quot; mode, for your gums, and \\&quot;Go Care\\&quot; mode, for those that need to finish quick. The rubber part tends to mold and it gets smelly.     <li> Packaging: The packaging of the device was appealing and organized. The pictures and the white coloring definitely inspired cleanliness. The listing of the features of the toothbrush on the box was short but still informative.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Portability: The toothbrush comes with a travel case that is compact enough to fit in most toiletry bags, however, the brush head needs to be detached in order to fit in it. The charger is very compact and readily able to travel.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Operation: The R910 features 3 brush modes: clean, sensitive, and massage. The clean mode was very intense and takes a little while to get used to, however, once you do it is definitely the most effective mode. It gets teeth very clean as the vibrations are at a significant amplitude. The sensitive mode is a good compromise if you have very sensitive gums. While it does not clean as thoroughly as the clean mode does, it is sufficient for daily use. I would personally not recommend using the massage mode. I felt that it did not make my gums any less sensitive than the sensitive mode, but the difference in cleanliness was noticeable. The R910 also featured timers and personalizeable routines which I didn&#x27;t find useful.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Overall: Overall, I would say that the toothbrush is very good.     <li> Engineers look at functionality in a simplistic mechanical way.&lt;br /&gt;Designers look for artful symmetry in design.&lt;br /&gt;Both failed to collaborate to design a functional piece that takes into account human anatomy as well as basic physics. To place on/off/change buttons at the singular location, the fulcrum of our toothbrush lever, which is where the human hand needs to balance the forces of pushing against the teeth while still holding, shows a lack of creative collaborative thought.&lt;br /&gt;Offsetting them slightly to either side would solve the problem of accidentally hitting those buttons. Having a programmable disable feature once brushing begins is another solution.     <li> First, I don&#x27;t own this model because I don&#x27;t need the extra features.  I own a cheaper Sonicare with replaceable AA batteries.  I&#x27;ve read all over the places that people complaining the rechargeable products won&#x27;t hold the charge after a short period of time.  Products including wireless land line phones, cell phones, shavers, rechargeable Sonicare, rechargeable tools, ..., you name it.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;I bought a new wireless phone with 2 handsets several years ago.  Both of the handsets could not hold the charge after one month of usage.  Each handset has two AAA NiCd rechargeable batteries.  I took the batteries out and found out that one of the batteries in each handset has died. Replaced them and both handsets have been working fine until now. Yes, I&#x27;m an electrical engineer.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Here is my finding why the rechargeable batteries failed to hold the charge.&lt;br /&gt;1. Product manufacturers use the cheaper battery (usually NiCd) which has inferior material and no QC at all.&lt;br /&gt;2. NiCd has memory effect. Study by your own as what it is.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;How to use rechargeable products correctly:&lt;br /&gt;1. If the product comes with lemon batteries(like my case above), then the following does not help.&lt;br /&gt;2. Although Lithium and NiMn batteries are claimed to have no memory effect, I don&#x27;t 100% believe it.&lt;br /&gt;3. Regardless the type of batteries in the product, do NOT place it on the charger after each use.  Put is aside not charging.  Only charge it when the batteries are depleted.  Suppose a new rechargeable can be charged 1000 times, you use one count each time you rest it on the charger.  There is an explanation on chemical reaction with electricity but I don&#x27;t intend to go over with it here.&lt;br /&gt;4. Yes, the battery will loose power through its internal drain.  Still, do NOT charge until power is completely out.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Hope it helps.  I&#x27;m not going to answer questions here because I want to live a simple life.</li>
    <li> I has having some tooth sensitivity and someone recommended this tooth brush to me. Since I started using it my teeth don&#x27;t hurt anymore (I brushed too hard). Comes with one head, the brush, and the base. I would recommend this to anyone who is looking for an electric brush. The price might be a turn off but my mother has had the same base for 8 years now and it still works as it should!</li>
    <li> I tried the Sonicare and was really surprised by how powerful it is....even in the the \\&quot;sensitive\\&quot; setting. This just seems like too much. Saving time brushing will usually mean you are brushing too hard, and likely cause gum recession and tooth abrasion. You need to brush softly, but for between 2-3 minutes, 2-3 times/day (as well as flossing, which nothing can replace). \\&quot;No pain, no gain,\\&quot; does NOT apply to toothbrushing. I certainly would not recommend this for your teeth. I think if they can tone it down 75%, it might actually be good. The gum \\&quot;massager,\\&quot; mode makes no sense. Proper tooth and gingival care has more to do with the prevention of accumulation of plaque, etc., and \\&quot;massaging,\\&quot; the gum is just a great marketing idea. I think the only people who really need to use an electric toothbrush are those with dexterity issues, etc., and they may benefit from the use of an electric toothbrush, but the trade off, e.g. recession, etc., may not be worth it. Also, you can&#x27;t really try to put less pressure on it, because then it will not clean that well. I can also tell you that you will spurt toothpaste all over unless you wait to turn it on when it is in your mouth...the vibration is just that harsh. If you accidently hit your tooth with it, and you will, you will feel like there is a jackhammer in your mouth! I know there are a lot of people out there who are huge fans of these....I am not one of them. If you are still considering this item.....You only get one head, so if you want to share this (if you are a fan) you will have to spend more on another head. It does come with different colored rings so you can identify different brushes, but those rings slip around, perhaps because of the harsh vibration (I pressed pretty hard to put the two pieces together). The actual quality is very good, and it feels sturdy and not cheaply made. I just cannot recommend something that makes my teeth hurt after using it, no matter how it feels, or how cool it looks (and it does look pretty cool and hi-tech). For about two-three percent of the cost of the Sonicare (and no operating cost) you can have a superior instrument to keep your teeth and gums healthy.s look pretty cool and hi-tech). For about two-three percent of the cost of the Sonicare (and no operating cost) you can have a superior instrument to keep your teeth and gums healthy.</li>

More specifically, Europe uses 240-volts for their electrical outlets.  In the Unites States, we use 120 volts.  So, our existing Sonicare toothbrushes (designed for the Unites States) wouldn&#x27;t work in Europe.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;We purchase these new Sonicare toothbrushes (those that are the subject of this review) specifically to use for our trip to Europe.  We&#x27;re pleased to say that these toothbrushes worked very well in Europe.  Essentially, they worked the same as our U.S. toothbrushes worked in the U.S.  When we returned to the U.S., we discovered that they work equally well on U.S.  That is, they work very well on both U.S.  voltage (120 volts) and European voltage (240 volts).&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;So, the big advantage of these toothbrushes, besides their excellent tooth-cleaning ability, is that they will work in most countries of the world. The cheesy base this comes with slides all over the counte due to the chintzy rubber \\&quot;anti-skid\\&quot; pads that Sonicare chose to specify in order to save 2 cents per brush.  I had some small, clear, 3M rubber pads on hand and solved the problem, after cleaning the base with rubbing alcohol.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Why should I have to do this with a toothbrush in this price level?  My original Sonicare&#x27;s base remained in place for a decade.  It also used a \\&quot;plastic ring design that made reinserting the brush into the charging pad a cinch. Not so with this one, which uses a relatively tiny charging \\&quot;male\\&quot; that inserts into the female base of the brush.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;This brush offers five different brushing routines, but isn&#x27;t \\&quot;smart\\&quot; enough to remember the last chosen setting.  Hence, it must be reset each time you brush unless the default setting is your preferred setting.  Additionally, the mode select switch (silver color) is poorly positioned and is prone to being inadvertently switched to another brushing mode during use.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;A well designed brush would include a \\&quot;lock\\&quot; for the mode select switch, which would \\&quot;remember\\&quot; and lock the last setting and require 5 - 10 seconds of continuous pressing prior to being unlocked.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;This brush is not a well thought out and thoroughly engineered product and I&#x27;m thinking about returning it.  People should be fired for this.</li>
